  gerundive in agreement with a noun, and uses the gerund as here. Meissner
  notes that Latin has no verb with the sense 'to see again', which a
  modern would use here. — <b>conscripsi</b>: in the <i>Origines</i>.
  — <b>quo</b>: = <i>ad quos</i>; see <a href="#Snot_12" class="pginternal">n. on 12</a>
<i>fore unde</i>. — <b>Pelian</b>: a mistake of Cicero's. It was
  not Pelias but his half-brother Aeson, father of Iason, whom Medea made
  young again by cutting him to pieces and boiling him in her enchanted
  cauldron. She, however, induced the daughters of Pelias to try the same
  experiment with their father; the issue, of course, was very different.
  Plautus, Pseud. 3, 2, 80 seems to make the same mistake. — <b>si
  quis deus</b>: the present subjunctive is noticeable; strictly, an
  impossible condition should require the past tense, but in vivid passages
  an impossible condition is momentarily treated as possible. So Cic.
  generally says <i>si reviviscat aliquis</i>, not <i>revivisceret</i>.
  — <b>decurso spatio</b>: 'when I have run my race'. See <a href="#Snot_14" class="pginternal">n. on 14</a>. Lucretius 3, 1042 oddly has <i>decurso
  lumine vitae</i>. — <b>ad carceres a calce</b>: <i>carceres</i>
  were the barriers behind which the horses and cars stood waiting for the
  race; <i>calx</i> (<span title="grammê" lang="el">γραμμη</span>), literally 'a
  chalked line', was what we should call 'the winning post'. Cf. Lael. 101;
  Tusc. 1, 15 <i>nunc video calcem ad quam cum sit decursum, nihil sit
  praeterea extimescendum.</i></p>
<p><b><a id="Snot_84"></a><a href="#Sect_84" class="pginternal">84.</a> habeat</b>:
  concessive. A. 266, <i>c</i>; G. 257; H. 484, 3. — <b>multi et ei
  docti</b>: as Nägelsbach, Stilistik § 25, 5, remarks, Cic. always
  uses this phrase and not <i>multi docti</i>. One of the books Cic. has in
  view is no doubt that of Hegesias, a Cyrenaic philosopher, mentioned in
  Tusc. 1, 84. — <b>commorandi ... divorsorium</b>: 'a hostelry
  wherein to sojourn'. The idea has been expressed in literature in a
  thousand ways. Cf. Lucr. 3, 938 <i>cur non ut plenus vitae conviva
  recedis</i>; Hor. Sat. 1, 1, 118 <i>vita cedat uti conviva satur</i>.
  Cicero often insists that heaven is the <i>vera aeternaque domus</i> of
  the soul (cf. Tusc. 1, 118). Cf. Epist. to the Hebrews, 13, 14 'Here have
  we no continuing city, but we seek one to come'. — <b>concilium
  coetumque</b>: so in Rep. 6, 13 <i>concilia coetusque hominum quae
  civitates vocantur</i>. The words here seem to imply that the real
  <i>civitas</i> is above; what seems to men a <i>civitas</i> is merely a
  disorganized crowd.</p>
<p><b>P. <a href="#Page_35" class="pginternal">35</a> — Catonem meum</b>: see <a href="#Sect_15" class="pginternal">15</a>, <a href="#Sect_68" class="pginternal">68</a>; so Cicero in his
  letters often calls his own son <i>meus Cicero</i>. — <b>nemo
  vir</b>: see <a href="#Snot_21" class="pginternal">n. on 21</a> <i>quemquam senem</i>.
  — <b>quod contra</b>: = <span title="ho tounantion" lang="el">‛ο
  τουναντιον</span>,
  'whereas on the contrary'; cf. n. on Lael. 90 where, as well as here,
  many of the editors make the mistake of taking <i>quod</i> to be the
  accusative governed by <i>contra</i> out of place. — <b>meum</b>:
  <i>sc. corpus cremari</i>. — <b>quo</b>: put for <i>ad quae</i>, as
  often. — <b>visus sum</b>: 'people thought I bore up bravely'.
  — <b>non quo ... sed</b>: a relative clause parallel with a
  categorically affirmative clause. The usage is not uncommon, though Cic.
  often has <i>non quo ... sed quia</i>. For mood of <i>ferrem</i> see A.
  341, <i>d</i>, Rem.; G. 541, Rem. 1.; H. 516, II. 2.</p>
<p><b><a id="Snot_85"></a><a href="#Sect_85" class="pginternal">85.</a> dixisti</b>: in <a href="#Sect_4" class="pginternal">4.</a>
  — <b>qui</b>: here = <i>cum ego</i>, 'since I ...'. —
  <b>extorqueri volo</b>: <a href="#Snot_2" class="pginternal">n. on 2</a> <i>levari volo</i>.
  — <b>minuti philosophi</b>: for the word <i>minutus</i> cf. <a href="#Snot_46" class="pginternal">n. on 46</a>; Cic. has <i>minuti philosophi</i> in Acad.
  2, 75; Div. 1, 62; in Fin. 1, 61 <i>minuti et angusti (homines)</i>; in
  Brut. 265 <i>m. imperatores</i>; cf. Suet. Aug. 83 <i>m. pueri</i>.
  — <b>sentiam</b>: future indicative. — <b>peractio</b>: the
  noun is said to occur only here in Cic.; cf. however <a href="#Sect_64" class="pginternal">64</a> <i>peragere</i>; <a href="#Sect_70" class="pginternal">70</a>.
  — <b>haec ... dicerem</b>: the same words occur at the end of the
  Laelius; for <i>habeo quod dicam</i> Cic. often says <i>habeo dicere</i>,
  as in Balb. 34.</p>
<hr><div class="pg_body_wrapper"><a id="Footnotes"></a></div><h2>Footnotes.</h2>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_1"></a><a href="#NtA_1" class="pginternal">[1]</a> Horace, Ep. 2, I, 156:— <br>
        <i>Graecia capta ferum victorem cepit, et artes <br>
        Intulit agresti Latio.</i></p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_2"></a><a href="#NtA_2" class="pginternal">[2]</a> De Off. 1, 1, 2: <i>philosophandi scientiam concedens multis</i> etc.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_3"></a><a href="#NtA_3" class="pginternal">[3]</a> To judge rightly of Cicero it must be remembered that he was a politician only by accident: his whole natural bent was towards literature.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_4"></a><a href="#NtA_4" class="pginternal">[4]</a> To see the truth of this it is only necessary to refer for example to the weight given to the opinions of Cicero in the heated political discussions of the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_5"></a><a href="#NtA_5" class="pginternal">[5]</a> Almost every branch of learning was ranked under the head of Philosophy. Strabo even claimed that one branch of Philosophy was Geography.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_6"></a><a href="#NtA_6" class="pginternal">[6]</a> 2, 3 <i>interiectus est nuper liber is quem ad nostrum Atticum de senectute misimus.</i> No argument can be founded on the words <i>interiectus est</i>, over which the editors have wasted much ingenuity. They simply mean 'there was inserted in the series of my works'.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_7"></a><a href="#NtA_7" class="pginternal">[7]</a> See 2, 23.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_8"></a><a href="#NtA_8" class="pginternal">[8]</a> 14, 21, 3; 16, 3, 1; 16, 11, 3.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_9"></a><a href="#NtA_9" class="pginternal">[9]</a> See Att. 14, 21, 1.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_10"></a><a href="#NtA_10" class="pginternal">[10]</a> It was certainly not written, as Sommerbrodt assumes, in the intervals of composing the <i>De Divinatione</i>. The words in 2, 7 of that work—<i>quoniam de re publica consuli coepti sumus</i> etc.—point to the end of September or beginning of October, 44, when Cicero returned to Rome and began to compose his Philippic orations.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_11"></a><a href="#NtA_11" class="pginternal">[11]</a> § <a href="#Sect_1" class="pginternal">1</a>.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_12"></a><a href="#NtA_12" class="pginternal">[12]</a> It is perhaps not a mere accident that the prowess of L. Brutus <i>in liberanda patria</i> is mentioned in § <a href="#Sect_75" class="pginternal">75</a>. There may be a reference to the latest Brutus who had freed his country.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_13"></a><a href="#NtA_13" class="pginternal">[13]</a> In March, 45.</p>

<p class="note"><a id="Nt_21"></a><a href="#NtA_21" class="pginternal">[21]</a> Works on Old Age are said to have been written by Theophrastus and Demetrius Phalereus, either or both of which Cicero might have used. One passage in § <a href="#Sect_67" class="pginternal">67</a>, <i>facilius in morbos ... tristius curantur</i>, is supposed by many to have been imitated from Hippocrates; but the resemblance is probably accidental. Cf. De Off. 1, 24, 83.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_22"></a><a href="#NtA_22" class="pginternal">[22]</a> See § <a href="#Sect_2" class="pginternal">2</a>.</p>

<p class="note"><a id="Nt_25"></a><a href="#NtA_25" class="pginternal">[25]</a> As Cicero's intention was to set old age in a favorable light, he slights Aristo Cius for giving to Tithonus the chief part in a dialogue on old age. See § <a href="#Sect_3" class="pginternal">3</a>; cf. also Laelius, § 4.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_26"></a><a href="#NtA_26" class="pginternal">[26]</a> See below <a href="#Page_xxiii" class="pginternal">(ii.), 1.</a></p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_27"></a><a href="#NtA_27" class="pginternal">[27]</a> On the whole subject of Aristotle's dialogues see Bernays' monograph, <i>Die Dialoge des Aristoteles</i>.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_28"></a><a href="#NtA_28" class="pginternal">[28]</a> § <a href="#Sect_32" class="pginternal">32</a> <i>quartum ago annum et octogesimum</i>. Cf. Lael. 11 <i>memini Catonem ante quam est mortuus mecum et cum Scipione disserere</i> etc.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_29"></a><a href="#NtA_29" class="pginternal">[29]</a> Cicero always indicates this date; cf. § <a href="#Sect_14" class="pginternal">14</a>. Some other writers, as Livy, give, probably wrongly, an earlier date.</p>

Cf. Gell. <i>Noct. Att.</i> 13, 23.</p>

<p class="note"><a id="Nt_34"></a><a href="#NtA_34" class="pginternal">[34]</a> If Plutarch may be trusted, Cato at the age of 30 had won for himself the title of 'the Roman Demosthenes'.</p>

<p class="note"><a id="Nt_36"></a><a href="#NtA_36" class="pginternal">[36]</a> In § <a href="#Sect_10" class="pginternal">10</a> Cicero makes the quaestorship fall in 205, but he refers to the election, not to the actual year of office.</p>

<p class="note"><a id="Nt_38"></a><a href="#NtA_38" class="pginternal">[38]</a> Cato afterwards made it a charge against M. Fulvius Nobilior that he had taken Ennius with him on a campaign (Tusc. 1, 3). But Cato used Ennius as soldier while Nobilior employed him as poet.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_39"></a><a href="#NtA_39" class="pginternal">39</a> It is difficult, however, to fix the date of this enactment. Some authorities place it after Cato's return from Spain.</p>
<p class="note"><a id="Nt_40"></a><a href="#NtA_40" class="pginternal">[40]</a> Livy 34, cc. 1-8.</p>

M. <a href="#Sect_32" class="pginternal">c 10</a> says <i>tribunus militaris</i>.</p>
